  In this moment, I need her to know the seriousness of what has happened without causing a scene. She is our designated driver this night which means no drinking, but we were nearly always high while driving.
  She laughs, “Hold on. We will go in a little while. They are rolling a blunt right now. Here, smoke with us.” She pats the cushion next to her. I am high and drunk as can be, but fear has sobered me.
  Knowing that I am the anxious one when we are out, she probably thinks my usual nerves are kicking in rather than something actually being wrong. Parties cause tremors throughout my entire body no matter how many substances I use. The fear can’t be buried and she is right, there isn’t always a known cause.
  Thinking back, I know it was a fear of being near drunk males, getting into trouble by the police, or something erratic happening because the environment felt eerily similar to my own family and past traumas.
  Fear of punishment by authority figures.
  Fear of sexual abuse by men who wouldn’t control themselves.
  Fear of abandonment by family members or close party friends.
  Fear of people objectifying my body.
  At the time, I didn’t make all these connections but just felt embarrassed at the inability to relax like my friends.
  “No Shannon, I’m being serious. That guy that I told you about just waited for me outside the bathroom and shoved me against the wall. He kept kissing me, and I begged him to stop but he wouldn’t. He was going to rape me but his mom came out and helped me. We need to go. Now.” My heart pounds so fast it throbs in my head.
  “Ugh, okay, we will go just after I finish smoking.” She looks annoyed, as if I have inconvenienced her fun.
  “Well, I need to get out of this house. Give me your keys, I’m going to wait in the car.” I'm annoyed at her lack of care, but reflecting as an adult, this was true to her behavior. The friendship was a continued cycle of abusive relationship dynamics I experienced within my own family.
  Thinking back, going to the car alone wasn’t a smart choice. The house was suffocating me into an anxiety attack where fear became overwhelming, breathing got heavy, limbs trembled uncontrollably, dizziness set in with a feeling of detachment, followed by an unrelenting chest pain. The same episodes I had when Mom would abandon me as a kid--or when someone would vomit. What others thought was me overreacting and being annoying, was actually intense anxiety attacks that I was experiencing regularly.
  I hope that going to the car will speed Shannon up, but also need to disappear and calm my anxiety away from the gaze of others.
  The guy instantly follows me outside, grabbing my hand as he walks by. I turn back toward the door but as my hand tries to escape, he yanks with such force that I fall down the iced steps. As I frantically crawl away with him grabbing at me, his mom appears from the doorway, buying my freedom to rush back into the mobile home.
  To my left, Shannon smokes on the couch and on my right, another friend Harper plays beer pong in the kitchen. Harper is smart and logical, she will help. I am certain.
  After sharing my experience, she links our arms and holds me close. We snatch empty beer bottles from the kitchen table to keep as protection, should anyone follow us. Then, we lock ourselves in the car while waiting for Shannon. The man and his friend stand outside the vehicle door, but we crunch onto the floor, attempting to disappear in the shadows.
  That night, we made it out safely. The strange man nearly got the sexual encounter he wanted with me through using aggressive force, what I now know would be considered sexual assault. There is so much I didn’t understand about that incident at the time. There were no connections made with my childhood abuse because Josiah wasn’t aggressive or forceful during times when he was touching me. No one taught me how to keep my body safe or what to do if I were assaulted, with either a stranger or someone I knew. I realized, of course, that the assault was wrong, but it never occurred to me to call the police on this stranger at a party, just like it never once occurred to me to tell the authorities or any adult about my brother.

  As junior year rolls around, something changes in me. No longer desiring the freedom of doing what I want, my anxiety craves routine and expectations. The chaos is too overwhelming.
  Dad has recently remarried and from what I know, my stepmom, Sylvia, seems enjoyable. She is a nurse at a hospital and owns a well kept house, with hobbies of gardening in the backyard and painting birdhouses.
  Although Dad rarely contacts us kids, there was a shift in him after the divorce. He sought therapy, drinks less, and to my knowledge, has never again laid a hand on any of us. Maybe he was only mean because of a toxic marriage, I hope, but these are only words Dad has fed me. His excuse for the abuse. Now, desperate for family normalcy, I long for them to be true so at the age of sixteen, I move in with him and Sylvia. At first, it almost feels normal. Each day, I receive a text from Sylvia “Kelly, will you be home tonight? If so, I will make you dinner!” Even though she doesn’t eat dinner herself, she always cooks me fresh meals when home. Dad also cares about my whereabouts. He texts stuff like “I need to know where you are Kelly,” and although I can imagine the critical tone, it doesn’t matter because he cares enough to text. He thinks of me and I ache for these small gestures like a baby yearns for its mother.
  During this time, I isolated myself from friends. Between school, soccer, and waitressing, my schedule was busy, which gave good excuses. My friends knew better and reached out with messages of love and concern with their lack of hearing from me. Shannon however, became particularly hurt by my disappearance, feeling betrayed and abandoned by me. I felt guilty for not having a better reason other than “I just want to be alone.” Taking care of my friends as I had with my Mom meant years of abandoning myself to care for others. I was exhausted. Not just exhausted, but deeply depressed.
  My cigarette and marijuana addictions have grown and I’m officially a high functioning “pothead,” smoking before school, at lunchtime, after school, before bed, and even more if I have the opportunity. Nothing compares to the end of the day when I return home high as a kite after Dad and Sylvia are in bed. On the living room couch with the wood burning stove still heating the house, I switch on one of four shows: Friends, Burlesque, SpongeBob or Tangled. Dad hates my shows, criticizing them anytime they are on, as he prefers that I watch the movies he showed me as a little girl about conspiracies, war, and doomsday but those scare me too much.
  So at night, while the rest of the world sleeps, I settle into myself. It is the only time I can let my guard down. The dark thoughts creep in and I welcome them, summoning the little girl inside of me who is desperate to be seen--to be heard, loved and valued. I feed her candy, chocolate milk, and her favorite shows. When she becomes scared, I smoke more, calming her pain and fears the only way I know how.

  “Hello?” My voice is tired and groggy.
  “Kelly?” She whispers, so quiet I could have imagined it.
  “Sarah? Is that you? What’s wrong? Is everything okay?!”
  “Can you please come pick me up?” As she speaks, I hears something in the background: George shouting and things slamming.
  “Yes, of course. I’m on my way. Where are you?! What’s happening?!”

  “Oh, Kelly, what is it?” He tries to be concerned, but even this sounds harsh. His tone is never right.

  Earlier that year, Mom and George had moved to a duplex with my younger siblings. It was so small that someone nearly always had to sleep on the living room couch or floor but after living in the trailer, this didn’t seem abnormal to anyone.

I enter cautiously, ready to press “call” on the “9-1-1” which is already dialed into my phone, but there is still no movement. Even the mice are silent. Once inside, I notice a hole the size of a fist in the bathroom door, with a bigger one below. I will later learn that the bathroom door was smashed in by George while Sarah hid from him on the other side.

Somehow, she is in the first place I check, tucked as far back into a deep closet as she can get. It’s exactly where I would have hidden. Digging through clothes, I reach her.

You can come out now.” She is ten years old now, but in this moment, I see her as the vulnerable toddler who needed my protection for so many years. Her gentle cries and insecure body show that she feels like the little girl I imagined. She crawls into my arms and I hold her shivering body close to mine. Everyone is safe, physically, but the emotional trauma remain.

There is no reason for George to be breaking shit and hurting people. If anyone owes someone an apology, it’s him! What is wrong with you Mom?!” I know that shouting, saying “God” and cursing will piss her off--and I don’t care. Remarks also came about how much of a “bitch” I was for being assertive with Mom. I was not having sex with multiple people but they were right about one thing, the undeniable hatred that spewed out when Mom angered me. She brought out my worst side and I wanted to hurt her in the same depth she hurt me. Maybe then she could understand and finally see me.

He really only gets mad when he drinks too much wine. Maybe I made a mistake. Maybe this is God punishing me for getting a divorce from your dad. This really is all my fault. I mess everything up. Nobody really loves me…” Abe arrives just in time to take over. Feeling annoyed and ready to leave, I invite any siblings who want to remain safe. It is the same game, but a different day. It was, what Mom is most talented at and what I had called, the biggest “mind fuck”. Yet I always caved and went along with it, attempting to soak up any small feelings of affection, which wasn’t all fake. I did empathize with how difficult it must be to raise so many children alone with limited resources while experiencing abuse. When we were best friends, I really felt her love, desiring to hold the moments forever while knowing that no matter what they wouldn’t last because at any given moment, she would return to a state of anger, manipulation, and overall unpredictability.

  The first few months at Dad’s were fine, until they weren’t. One day, he raged at me. I cannot remember what it was about, maybe chores or forgetting to text him my whereabouts.
  As he shouts, I become cornered in the kitchen. Despite his heavy drinking, Dad is aging well and at fifty five years, he has few gray hairs, some faint wrinkles around his eyes, and still wears the same leather belt. Not much has changed. Like usual when he shouts, my head feels hazy and disconnected, unable to fully hear his words. Then it tunes back in, “And why do you always act like you’re scared of me or something?! Huh? Why Kelly? Why?” he barks, demanding an answer.
  For the first time with him, I gained a voice. Usually, I stuttered around Dad, especially when he was angry. In my mind--and I imagine in his--this proved that I really was a “stupid little retard” just as he said.
  This day, Looking straight into his dark eyes as he towers over me, I speak clear and steady, “I act like I’m scared of you, because I am scared of you Dad.” For the first time ever he sees a glimpse of me. Stuttering was instinctual, but this response is a choice. A vulnerable and honest choice.
  To my surprise, his face changes and almost softens. He steps back, pausing to think. It’s a look I haven’t seen before. Is he having a realization? Does he finally get it? I wonder. Not staying to receive an answer, I sprint to my room and lock the door, taking their dog with me to hide under the covers while my trembling body fights to regain steady breath.
  All the while thinking: He paused. He stepped back. He heard you, saw you, and listened. It gives me hope.
  Sometime later, I sit in a bubble bath. It is the only thing that stops the shivers that come with my anxiety. Suddenly, shouting sounds from the living room. Dad’s voice is clear but there is another male. “I’m gonna put you in a fucking ditch!” This man shouts, just as scary as Dad. Even in the bath, I freeze like a deer in headlights, ready to hide if need be. That is how on guard I always feel. When the shouting stops, I exit the bath and dress quickly, curious to know what happened.

Then, he will no longer be angry toward me about anything, I convince myself. If only I perfect this testimony, then I can be loved.

On the stand, I answer the questions asked in front of the strangers. When probed about my perspective of what happened, I tell them. “Well, I was in the bath and heard yelling but it didn’t sound like my dad so...” Having prepared for hours and days, I finish, answering any and all of their questions with complete confidence. I perform even better than I wanted, I was perfect.

That was you telling them that you’re used to hearing me yell! You told them I’m a mean person! You are the reason they didn’t give me the protection order!” He rages.

  I wanted to believe his anger came from a toxic marriage but instead, after just turning seventeen, I knew that dysfunction married dysfunction. And they had a bunch of kids. What would that mean for me?

Chapter 23

Burned


  In late winter of my junior year, George announced a last-minute extravagant-for-us spring break vacation. On a weekend visit home, before they had moved into the duplex, we stood in the spacious dining room of the biggest house I had ever lived. For years he made it his goal to give us a rich life. On his painter’s salary, I knew that this house was above he and Mom’s pay grade. They were still against public schools, and pouring unholy amounts money into my little sibling’s private Lutheran education. At just barely seventeen, I knew they were wasting money that they did not have on this vacation.
  Regardless, excitement grew as I anticipated my first ever real vacation. The type of trip that some of my friends went on regularly with their families.
  “Ve arrre going de be on de beach and eat expensive foood!” George’s Hungarian accent is strong. His green eyes glow the way that Mom’s do when she acts on impulses. We are to spend a week in Florida with an added cruise trip to the Bahamas.
  “Yes, I am so excited!” Jittery with emotion, I am already planning my wardrobe, hair, and makeup styles.
  Enjoying my excitement, George approaches for a hug and a sadistic smile forms on his face as he admires my body. I feel naked. My hands guard the front of my chest, preparing to shove him away. Instead, I freeze and let out an uncomfortable laugh while he kisses my face repeatedly. His prickly facial hairs scratch like sandpaper and I’m Gagging at the strong stench of body odor, desperate to scrub myself in the shower the moment it’s over.

  In Florida, we park outside the fanciest hotel that I have ever seen. “This is not the hotel that we are staying at. We just need to run in and talk to some people about a timeshare," Mom explains. The lobby has spotless bright red carpet, shimmering chandeliers, and a flawless view of the Atlantic Ocean.

Upon returning, Mom instructs us to put on our swimsuits. “They have a beautiful outdoor pool and beach that you can swim in while waiting for us!” We are directed past the pool and to the beach. It is beautiful and we share excitement for our first ocean experience. “We will not be gone long, I promise! We will be back in an hour," mom assures us.
  My siblings and I stare at each other awkwardly, uncertain what to do. With me being the oldest, I take initiative in scoping the area to make a plan. It is about 9am and the pool is already crowded with swimmers and guests at the minibar. The beach being more open feels easier for us to stay together safely. We pick a spot in the sand, slap on a quick coat of sunscreen and head for the water.
  Hours pass and despite my calls and texts, there has been no word from Mom and George.
  Rotating between the beach and pool, we fill the first few hours with made up games, picture taking, people watching, and chattering about what to expect on a cruise ship. By afternoon, we all feel bored, wandering aimlessly trying to manage our worries and frustrations about being left alone in an unfamiliar place.
  Hunger and thirst overcome the boredom as we ache to see our guardians again. My sister and I take turns wandering inside in search of Mom and George without success. Finally, at about 4:30pm, they return.
  “Mom, where were you guys?” Irritation rattles in my voice.
  “Did you have fun?! We can start our vacation now!” She exclaims.

  “Let’s go to our hotel and get dinner.” Mom and Georges smiles show that they are not acknowledging our anger.
  Sarah and I glance at each other, sharing eye rolls. What the heck is wrong with them? Silent words transfer between us.
  Mom and George walk with excitement, oblivious about abandoning their four children for seven hours in a strange place with no food, water, or money while they were undoubtedly wined and dined in a condo sales pitch.
  Piling into the clunky minivan, George says that he will be stopping for wine. He has set his mind to a specific type of drink that he struggles to find, despite searching multiple stores. My sunburn felt excruciatingly hot and my bones ache. With no air conditioning in the van, sweat dribbles down my legs.
  “Oh my God, seriously? You’re stopping at another store?” My weariness grows with exhaustion. This doesn’t feel like a vacation. So far, it seems like hell.
  “Yes, just one morrre storrre.”
  “You literally said that three stores ago… but sure, don’t worry about us so long as you have your stupid wine.” My eyes close and I cover my face in attempts to soothe a pulsing headache.
  Before fully processing my words, George plummets into an unmanageable rage. “You vill not talk to me and yourrr Mommy that way! Who do you dink you arrre?! Cahming on ourrr vacation and talking to us vit no rrrespect!”
  “I am not trying to be rude. But seriously we are hot and tired a…”
  “Shut up! You arrre kid! You valk arrround dinking you’rrrre queen of vorld…” His words shift into unrecognizable Hungarian.
  When the van stops my mind returns from its fog. “Vit out! Vit out of my van right now!”
  “No.” I respond fearlessly.
  “I said vit out!” He demands.
  My eyes meet Mom’s, “Mom, I don’t have to get out of the car, right? I wasn’t trying to have an attitude! I am just really irritated and don’t feel well!”
  “Well, Kelly, you shouldn’t have gotten an attitude with him. Apologize and you can stay.”
  “What?! You’re taking his side?!” She never would have taken Dad’s side when he treated us like this. I would not have needed to scream at him in the first place because she would have taken that role for me. I hate George and what he has done to my mom. She makes excuses to justify his abuse yet chastises my attitude when I’m sick. “He literally just yelled so much worse than my attitude and is telling me to get out of the car. I will not apologize.”
  “Well, if you want to stay, you need to apologize.”
  “No, I am not apologizing. I hate you. I hate you so much Mom! I wish you had never given birth to me in the first place. I wish I was dead so that I never had to live in this stupid world with you.” My words are meant to hurt, as I’m searching for the sharpest sword to depict my feelings. If it stabs hard enough, maybe then she will understand the extent of my pain.
  “Vat did you just say to her?!” Turning in his seat, I look into the eyes of Satan himself.
  I will not be controlled.
  Spit spews through clenched teeth and raging words. His arms swat the air in attempts to whack me behind his seat but I’m quicker at dodging, cowering just out of his reach. When he slides the door beside me open, my feet and eyes return to the floor, afraid. No longer daring to look at him. “Don’t touch me George. I didn’t mean it. I really didn’t mean it. Please, I didn’t mean it. Just please don’t touch me.” I squirm as he reaches me.

  Grabbing my arm, he drags me from the van, gives a shove, returns to his seat, and leaves with my family.
  I sit on a curb in defeat attempting to control my tears. The sunburn still hurts and I’m unsure if my limbs are shaking from scorching physical pain or intense anxiety. Perhaps it is both.
  “Okay, you need to make a plan," I tell myself out loud while forcing slow deep breaths in and out. Looking up, I struggle to smell through my now stuffy nose. Finally, I feel the warm sun hit my face, followed by the light smell of fresh tar, a sure sign of regaining control. Scoping my surroundings, the parking lot is empty with a few run-down businesses. Traffic shuffles by behind me but otherwise, there’s not a single person in sight. I wonder if they will come back for me. If not, I will walk somewhere to get help. I will call Dad. He will freak when he hears how another man treated his daughter. I strategize.
  The thoughts of them not returning brings shivers. Hugging my legs closely, I rest my head on my knees and allow silent tears to flow again. Being abandoned in a vacant parking lot is not what I had imagined for the first day of my first vacation.
  Ten to fifteen minutes later, they return and mom exits the van to sit next to me. Yes, I think, Now I have her alone. I can make her understand and she will comfort me.
  “You need to go apologize to George.” My thoughts are interrupted.
  “What, are you kidding me?! No, Mom. I will not apologize to him.”
  “Well, that is the only way that he will let you back in the car.”
  “Then I will find my own way home because I am not apologizing to him.”
  “Look Kelly, I do not know why George gets angry like this. But I do know that you should not have gotten an attitude with him. You need to tell him that you are sorry.”
  Eventually, Mom caves, and I am allowed back into the van.
  At the hotel, George packs his bags and mom catches me alone. “George says that he is buying a plane ticket home and will only stay if you apologize. Just go tell him you are sorry Kelly. Your attitude is ruining this vacation.”
  We debate and compromise, I would apologize to George if he also apologizes to me. Mom agrees, only if I go first. With shame and embarrassment I fight every instinct in me to force the words out. “I am sorry for getting an attitude with you George.” I speak, just above a whisper.
  “Oh Kelly, I love you! Come herrre and give me kisses.” I step back but not quick enough to escape his lips.
  My apology from him was never given.

~


  The first day on the cruise ship feels surreal. I am uncertain as to whether it is from being on the ship itself, or because sickness and exhaustion from the sunburn have put me in a permanent dreamlike state. We arrive at our tiny ship cabin that has two half-sized bunk beds. To our relief, Mom and George have a private room of their own.
  I remove my flip phone from my pocket and plop onto a bottom bunk. At the age of fifteen, two years prior, I began waitressing at West Haven’s local Chinese restaurant. My boss, Jenn, had begun playing a motherly role in my life. Being on the ocean means that I can not have contact with her for multiple days due to lack of cellular connection. My chest is heavy as I anticipate being away from my safety net, both friends and Jenn. I send quick text updates, leaving out the tragic events from the day before. Desperate to not overwhelm her, I speak of the beauty on the cruise ship and send lots of love.
  Staring out the cabin window, I can’t help but notice my burning skin, throbbing head, and aching body. “Common’, let’s go explore and find the pool!” Sarah, Daniel, and Avalon plead with excitement. This is potentially my only chance of being on a cruise ship. A once in a lifetime experience. Eager to enjoy it, I shove my pain aside to engage in all of the activities with a sense of happiness.
  By the time the cruise ship returns, massive blisters have formed on my face and arms, one being so big that it takes up the entirety of my forehead. Constant nausea makes eating unthinkable. My body screams for rest, but the burning skin will not allow me to even touch the bedsheets. Cool baths lose the battle in reducing the heat on my skin, a the water turns instantly hot. I continue shaking with feverish chills as I stand naked in the bathroom. Nothing feels comfortable, everything hurt. I know that I need a hospital but do not dare speak it, for every emotion must remain inside, buried deep.

Chapter 24

Corbin


  That spring I moved back in with Grandma Williams, who welcomed me with open arms. Mom had desired me to help with the siblings and Dad aimed to control. Grandma suffered from loneliness so I kept her company when able. With having a baby to care for, Lesley, Josiah's wife, required a sitter. Every living option came with terms and although earning my place was understandable, no one truly saw me. Grandma Williams’ became the best choice due to our mutual desire for connection.
  Two of my friends, Shannon and Cassie assisted with the move from Dad’s. I imagine moving abruptly hurt he and Sylvia, but didn’t have the words for what I was experiencing at the time, aside from an urgency to disappear.
  Back at Grandma’s, things continued as usual. Each morning she woke at 5am and switched the news on, which had primarily bad reports, which created a similar discomfort to when Dad spoke of government conspiracies. After catching her morning report, she headed for the computer to play solitaire. Although she had some piano students, most of her energy was poured into quilting and writing. “I am writing a novel! It is about a Romanian man falling in love with an American woman!” Her smile widened as she spoke of her work. For many years, she had dated a Romanian man, adoring his accent and accordion-playing skills. The hypnotizing way she spoke of him reminded me of how Mom looked at George, leaving a pit in my stomach. “My novel has too many adult scenes though so you cannot read it until you are an adult," she explained. To my knowledge, it was never finished, but I imagine the act of writing provided purpose for her life throughout those years.
  Around this time I had begun a similar journey--the search for purpose, which first required belonging. Friends filled this need, with one thing setting me apart, they were all having sex. They looked at males with googly eyes and kept tallies of each one they slept with, raving about who had the best orgasm. Not only did I not desire sex, but struggled to engage in the conversations.

He had a traumatic childhood of his own in which we bonded over throughout the years. The first years, he called constantly and we stayed up many nights talking until early mornings. At school, he caught me between classes, adoring little things about me, like the way I wore my hair, always delighted to see me. I loved him and the attention he gave me, but especially adored our connection--one that I didn’t realize at the time was rooted in trauma and codependency.
  Being less than two years apart in age, Abe and I had some mutual friend groups and he proudly wore the “protective big brother” title. One day, he picked me up from school and on the drive, we slid off an iced road straight into a snowbank. It happened so slowly that there was no time time for fear, just thankful that it wasn’t worse.
  “Get out and push from the front while I hit the gas pedal to get us unstuck. Here, you push like this when you hear the engine revving. Got it?” He was kind in adolescence and took the time to teach me things, such as this.
  Yet the moment I started dating Corbin, my brothers, particularly Abe, verbalized disapproval. His tone was lighthearted, but the words were foul. “So what, you’re a nigger-lover now?” He shook his head in disbelief, sighed, and giggled.
  “What?! No! Corbin isn’t like that.” During our childhoods, Dad trained us thoroughly on his stance of other races. Black and brown people were “rapists,” “lowlifes,” “thugs,” or “terrorists”. I knew that these perceptions were where Abe’s mind was, but had never met someone with another skin color who fit the criteria Dad spoke of. Especially not Corbin.
  “He’s great! You just have to get to know him.” I tried remaining hopeful but doubted this explanation made any difference. It was all so confusing and didn’t make sense why my brother rejected someone so special to me.
  “I don’t know how else he expects people to look at him with cornrows, baggy clothes, and sagging pants. He’s a thug,” he grumbled back. I loathed Abe for this and wanted to defend Corbin. Yet I trusted my brother and this perception created insecurity when in Corbin’s presence, as if I didn’t notice something bad about him that the rest of the world saw.
  “You’re grandma and dad are racist," Mom always said. “It is because your dad grew up in Benton Harbor surrounded by black people and they weren’t very nice to him. It sounds like he got beat up a lot,” Dad shared similar stories, also using the word “nigger.” At the time, I barely knew the meaning—just that it was really bad. The conflict between these two worlds of my family versus what I felt in my core to be right was paralyzing. It was yet another experience where that created doubt on whether I could trust myself.
  As Corbin and I’s relationship continued, he had a lot to process with his own trauma. Although honored that to have his trust, I eventually came to feel like a therapist. So much so that he even called me his therapist. It was a compliment, aside from needing someone to meet my emotional needs to the same depth I met theirs. He had poor boundaries with other girls, which made me uncomfortable. The more he dismissed my feelings of his interactions with others, the more distant we became.
  “Have I ever done anything to show that you shouldn’t trust me? Have I ever lied to you?” He sounded annoyed and irritated at signs of my vulnerability, leaving knots in my stomach. His responses felt similar to Mom’s treatment of me, leaving little space for any emotion that didn’t revolve around him.
  “Well, no, but the way you interact with those girls makes me uncomfortable…” I didn’t yet know the words for, “You dismiss my feelings repeatedly. That shows that I can’t trust you.” The conversations ended when I caved, abandoning myself by believing that he was right and I just needed more confidence.

Thinking back, he was a young boy who had experienced the unthinkable and found a girl who listened intently, loving him anyway. Knowing the complexity of attachment issues that I do now, I imagine that breaking his connection to me was hard, if not nearly impossible for him. Everyone, even random parents and teachers believed we were a match made in heaven, two personalities meant to find each other. Two kids from broken homes persevering through adolescence together. Still, no one understood us. Even when he did attempt supporting me emotionally, I hadn’t known how to let him. The fact that I still didn’t desire sex with him made this lack of connection all the more prevalent.

I have been on birth control for a while, hoping to ease nerves, yet still avoid sex. “Okay, let's do something else now. Like get ice cream and drive to the beach," I will say, finding any reason to get away from it.

My hands reach his six pack and while he stares with gentle brown eyes, waiting from my approval, all I can think, Flawless. This man is flawless. I pull him into a hug wishing to remain in the safety of each other's arms forever. He can’t reach me emotionally, but would never physically hurt me. He is a protector, choosing me over and over again. Never, in a million years, would he let someone lay a hand on me. He’s a man and I’m not afraid of him. If I do not want sex with him, then I am truly broken, I have convinced myself, yet refuse to become a victim to my own body.
  “Yes, keep going, just keep going.” I state, unconvincingly.
  “Are you sure you want this? Like, really sure?” He appears nervous, which is confusing because of the amount of people he has already had sex with during our times apart.
  “Yes, just stop asking and keep going.”
  Finally, he accepts my words and continues.
  Feeling this close to someone is overwhelming making it hard to breathe correctly. I feel trapped, yet push through the fear in hopes that getting this whole sex thing over with will “fix” me.
  A bit later, a knock sounds on the bedroom door. It is Jenn’s youngest daughter, Sam, who has just gotten off her school bus.
  “Corbin! Do you have any of that candy left?” she shouts.
  When he pauses to answer, my eyes catch the clock beside the bed giving the perfect excuse. “Uhm, I have to go to work. I need to go.”
  “Hold on, just a little longer. I’ll tell Sam to come back later.” He is determined to make sure I feel comfortable but doesn’t know that no matter his efforts, it is impossible. My body won’t let me feel safe with that level of intimacy.
  “No Corbin, I have to go. I’m sorry, but I can’t be late for work. Really, I have to go!” I feel frantic.

My brothers have said I dress as a “tease” with my jeans and makeup and this would confirm their accusations. Having sovereignty over my body wasn’t a notion that my family instilled in me. Instead, they viewed such acts as “mean,” “sinful,” or “insensitive”. At the age of seventeen, I hadn’t even known the word “boundaries” outside of athletics.
  At work, one thought remains: Sex feels familiar. Why? The next year, in a psychology course, I would learn about how our minds can repress memories to protect us from the intensity of emotional trauma. Sometimes a flashback, nightmare, or body memory can resurface what’s buried and in this case, it was triggered by the sexual encounter.
  A few days later, while driving Shannon home, a panic attack begins and I’m suddenly sobbing, unable to breathe. It is the same heaving feeling that came after a childhood whooping from Dad’s leather belt.
  From the passenger seat, Shannon looks terrified “Are you okay?! What’s happening?!”

  “Woah! I have never seen someone be this high on weed and cry this hard.” She is trying to make a joke but it isn’t what I needed. It’s as if years of heavy weed-smoking has sucked up any amount of joy my brain is capable of creating. There are no more ingredients for the “happy recipe” in my head.

Yet some part of my body is screaming sex feels familiar because… And I can’t fight it down anymore, you were sexually abused.

I feel like I should share something personal about myself but the worst thing that has happened to me was drinking pee out of a mountain dew bottle on a family road trip as a kid.” She appears nervous, as if wanting to say just the right thing but not knowing what, so she goes for shared vulnerability. Her effort it appreciated, yet it’s still not what I need because drinking pee feels meaningless in comparison.
  Even so, we laugh about her drinking pee and the stress of the situation starts passing. Putting the car into drive, we move on as if nothing happened.
  For the following months, we never speak of my sexual abuse. The remainder of that summer is spent partying, working, trying to enjoy sex with Corbin and eventually other men. I bury the memories deep, hoping to ignore them until they no longer existed at all.

Chapter 25

Elizabeth


  As summer 2011 neared an end, I became even closer to my boss, Jenn. Throughout my two years working at the restaurant she had become like a mother to me with her love and acceptance. She never appeared annoyed by my talking or responded with unkind remarks. Instead, the more we spoke, the more she enjoyed my presence, making her more trustworthy than most in my life.
  One day, while clearing a table, I lose myself in conversation with Jenn. “I think I might have been sexually abused by my brother Josiah.” Suddenly, my head feels dizzy just speaking the words. After years of burying the secret I’m now blabbering on about it for the second time.
  She looks up from her work, pushed the metal framed glasses up the bridge of her nose. “Oh, really?” then continues clearing dishes beside me. Other than the brief pause, her demeanor doesn’t change. She works just as quickly, her long blond ponytail swinging as we head toward the kitchen. “Have you thought about… I don’t know… talking to someone about that?” she asks. Similar to Shannon, I can tell that she wants to say just the right thing without fully knowing how.
  “Talk to someone… like who?”
  “Someone… I don’t know... like a therapist. You know? To help you sort it out.” Her voice perks up with the idea suggested.
  The confidence she portrays is comforting. “A therapist? Yes! That is such a good idea! I don’t know why I never thought of that! I’m going to ask my mom to help me find a therapist! Thank you!”
  Something about this plan feels right, although I’m not fully certain why. Over the months I ask Mom multiple times to help me find a counselor. “Why do you want a counselor?” Mom usually squints in confusion, as if the idea is absurd.
  “Because Mom, something’s not right with me and I think talking to someone could really help," I explain, attempting to convince her.
  When she doesn’t follow through, I plead. “Please Mom, you said you were going to help me!”
  “I know Kelly, you’re right. I will! I just haven’t gotten around to it yet. But I’ll put it on my to-do list,” This has been her response following each conversation.
  Irritated from the lack of concern, I snatch my Medicaid insurance card from her purse and begin a google search. Over the next few days I call multiple places, many of them at least thirty miles outside of West Haven, but none of them accept our government-run insurance. The potential referrals they give circle me back to places I have already called and as my hope was dwindles, risk-taking behaviors increase.
  On a mid October day I drive a car full of intoxicated friends around town while blaring music. It’s not even 2pm yet but the fun has started early for our senior year homecoming, “Oh my gosh guys, look at me! I’m smoking a bowl, with a drink and cigarette in hand, while driving. Talk about multitasking!” We all laugh and Shannon comments on how proud she is.
  For years my friends have called me “Monica.”
  “You are the anxious mom of the group. The one who takes care of us. Monica is a name that fits you well!” They decided it one night after I changed Shannon out of vomit-soaked clothes and yelled at her for taking pills that I had commanded her not to.

Instead, as I speed down a country road, seeing how fast Dad’s little black car goes, a thought repeats in my head: So what if I die? If I’m never going to be good enough, why try? It is a well thought-out plan to intentionally flirt with death.

Inside the school, students cheer as the fight song blares from speakers, everyone screaming our lungs out while feeling the noise of the crowd rumbling through our chests.

My mouth goes dry knowing what this must be about. The worst trouble I have gotten into at school was an occasional ‘tardy infraction’ after talking too long with friends in the hallway but never have I been called to the principal's office.

Just the year prior, a party was busted and everyone denied drinking when the cops asked, except for me, knowing that in my household, nothing compared to the whooping that came from a lie. To my relief, I got out of that charge, something about the cops being too rough and mishandling us teenagers.

She’s pretty drunk.” He pauses and steps closer, as if rubbing his power in my face. Or maybe this is just how it feels amidst my fear. “So I’m going to ask you one more time, no more lying. Did you drive Shannon to school today?”

He will hurt me worse than ever before for getting in trouble with the cops and at school! Weed and cigarette smoking continued as much as before, but only in the safety of certain friends' homes. Shannon and I were finished being friends, as the lifestyle we engaged in made me feel worse. After years of trying to help her as she sped down a road of reckless behavior and drugs I was now keenly aware of myself being pulled down a similar path. Taking risks with substances and longing for death wasn’t like me, which meant breaking things off with her to save myself.

  By senior year my grades had slightly improved and I was “dual-enrolled” in a few courses at the local community college. All of my friends were attending, so naturally, I joined to stay near them. The college, being built just eight years before was much nicer and clean than the high school, which had mold, mice, and ceiling leaks. My favorite course was Introduction to Psychology, taught by an adjunct professor, Elizabeth Harris. Her personality was gentle and down to earth; I could tell she genuinely valued and understood her students by the way she interacted with them. She sometimes spoke of her husband and three children during examples, and it seemed she lived a life I only dreamed of. As a young girl hours were spent on my scooter imagining the woman I would someday be. Eventually, this created a coping method: each time meeting someone, I searched for endearing and strong personality traits and applied them to myself. In the past, I struggled with school, but this foray into psychology made sense, making it possible to connect with the learning material in a new way. Even textbook readings made sense, always leaving me wanting to know more.

  “Okay, good. How are you feeling? What are some thoughts you’re having?” This is the first time it feels like someone truly cares about how I’m doing.
  “Elizabeth, is he going to go to jail? Are my siblings and I going to get taken away?”
  “I don’t know, Kelly. I am not an investigator and I cannot answer that.” We process a bit longer before ending. “Please reach out if you need anything and call that number I gave you to schedule a counseling session," she instructs.
  “Okay, I will.”
  Within five minutes, I arrive at Jami’s and update her on my day. “Oh my gosh Kelly, that’s so scary! You are so amazing. And so strong. If you need to stay here longer than a night or need a place to live I’m sure my mom won’t mind," she offers.
  “Thanks, I just… I don’t know… I don’t know what to expect.” I pace her basement, unable to sit still or catch any of my thoughts.
  Moments later, Mom calls “Kelly, the police are here and they are arresting Josiah. Okay Kelly? They handcuffed him and are arresting him right now. He’s being arrested. I thought you should know that.” Her voice is accusatory and I feel her anger.
  “What? Seriously?!” I can’t muster any other words. Many years later, I learned that when children’s protective services and the police arrived, Sarah disclosed her own sexual abuse during their questioning. She has since told me that Mom instructed her to lie so although enough detail was disclosed for a direct arrest, very little was shared concerning the extent of the actual abuse.
  “And they want me to ask you when they can talk to you tomorrow.” Mom says through the phone.
  “Uhm, tell them that I can’t talk tomorrow. I go straight from school to work so I can’t talk to them.”
  Mom hangs up and a short while later another call comes from a detective to schedule an interview at the police station.
  Texts are also pouring in from Mom saying things like:
  “You are so selfish. You know that Kelly? You need to learn to stop being so bitter and forgive.”
  “You have no idea what you’re putting me and this family through.”
  “God will punish you for sending your brother to jail out of anger. You are a bitter person Kelly.”
  “You are tearing this entire family apart. I hope you know that.”
  The texts continue one after another for hours into the night, just as similar messages from her will come for days, months, and even years following.
  I try defending myself with responses like: “But Mom, you promised you wouldn’t be mad at me. I’m really sorry. I didn’t mean to get anyone in trouble,” all to no avail. Eventually I convince myself that she will understand once the emotion from it all passes.
  I ramble while reading the messages to my friend. “What?! What is wrong with your mom! Kelly, this is not your fault!! I can’t believe she is saying stuff like that!” Jami looks ready to fight. I feel validated in her response and thankful to have someone understanding my perspective. I am not alone. She is a friend who will remain in my life through adulthood, sending me “thinking of you gifts” and encouraging notes for many years.
  There’s a feeling of desperation to scream, or maybe even destroy Jami’s basement, but smashing glass bottles on her parents’ minibar or throwing wall decorations through the window could just barely touch the amount of tension in my body. Instead, I shove those urges aside and message Elizabeth the updates. Again, she responds with the same compassion as in the classroom, saying things like, “Oh Kelly, I am so sorry that you are going through this. Please remember that this is not your fault. You are brave and strong and have done absolutely nothing wrong.”
  Repeating her words in my head, I hold the phone close, as if it makes them more true, and attempt to sleep. Each time I come remotely close to rest, my body jerks awake shaking uncontrollably. I have spoken about the abuse and now there is no turning back. Life itself will be forever changed.

Chapter 26

Interrogations


  It is a cool November day and the sun beams through clouds as I park my dad’s beat-up Prizm in the community college lot. Elizabeth is planning to drive me to the police station where I will give my statement regarding the sexual abuse. Feeling relieved that she hasn’t arrive yet, I smoke another cigarette.
  What am I doing? I ask myself, struggling to understand my situation. I had thought that talking to a counselor would help me, but is it worth the risk? Do I actually want Josiah to go to prison?
  My answer is No, of course I do not want Josiah to get into trouble. My sole focus has been trying to help myself and there hasn’t been thought about how that might mean hurting Josiah, Mom, my siblings, and many others.
  It is difficult to describe the experience of living a chaotic life of regular abuse and neglect, then realizing that the trauma has finally caught up to my mind, body, and spirit, only to have everything blow up when asking for help. At the age of seventeen, I do not know what it is like to identify or have my needs met. Instead, it feels that everyone belongs to this world but me and my sole reason for existing is to appease and please others. If done well enough, I might be given affection, but showing any of my own humanness puts me at risk of being a burden, rejected, or forgotten. Not receiving the love and nurturing that I needed most over the years has made me invisible--even to myself.
  The personal need of a counselor is evident only because death sounds more pleasant than life, which feels like a bigger issue. I now wonder if saving myself is worth the cost of hurting the people who I love the most. I want it to be, but the answer is no, so I inhale more smoke, desperate for relief.
  Elizabeth arrives in a small black car rather than the light blue minivan that she usually drives. I lather myself in lotion and spray scents onto my coat to hide the smell of smoke.
  “Sorry I’m late, I had to switch vehicles with my husband so that he could get the kids from school," she explains quickly, as if coming from a busy day. Then, she appears relieved having made it to me in time.
  Shortly into the drive, I stiffen and regret allowing someone to attend the questioning with me. Being alone feels safer. Outside, vibrant colored trees blur past, leaving me distant from the world as I wonder what to expect. What will the officers look like? What types of questions will they ask? Should I answer them? Should I lie like my mom has instructed? Could I answer them even if I tried? Will I have any words at all?
  “So, how are you feeling?” Elizabeth interrupts my thoughts.
  I tune in briefly and see her black sunglasses resting on small ears where pearl earrings dangle lightly. It’s relieving that eye contact isn’t required during a drive. “I’m feeling okay.” My eyes return to the side window, mind disappearing into leftover thoughts.
  After quick glance in my direction and back to the road, she probes on, “Your legs are shaking. What are some thoughts that you are having?”
  Suddenly I can’t remember the last time my limbs weren’t shaking. Even my bones ache from the strain. Tensing tighter, I channel the tremors back to my core where they can’t be seen. “I just don’t want to get Josiah into trouble and I don’t want my family to be mad at me.”

  Peering over, I focus on anything but her eyes.
  “No, I need you to really look at me and hear this.” A glance at the road and back to me, she pulls her sunglasses down. Looking into her eyes burns, as if she sees pieces of me that I don’t dare speak. The vulnerability is too much.
  She waits, silently, until my gaze meets hers.
  “This is not your fault. You have done nothing wrong. Nothing.” She pauses for another peek at the road. “Do not listen to anyone who tells you otherwise. This is not your fault. Okay?”

  I don’t believe a word she said and it is too early to know that the drive down this winding road will be the start of a soul binding friendship. My life's greatest gift.
  It is late afternoon when we arrive at the police station. Peering into the rearview mirror, there’s a store: Family Video. On weekends when I visit Mom it is where we rent movies and buy snacks for sleepovers with my friends. I had never noticed the police station across the parking lot or thought I would arrive here for disclosure statement.
  “Are you ready? Is there anything else that you want to talk about first?” Elizabeth’s face shows sincerity.
  “I don’t think so.” I take a deep breath, anxious to get this over with.

  Elizabeth turns, waiting for me to answer but I’ve lost the words.
  “She has an appointment to talk with an officer," she explains.
  Once checked in, we are instructed to sit in two chairs located by the door. The waiting room is small and uninspired. It reminds me of the jail where Mom often visited Glen when I was young. At the trailer, we were to ask an adult before answering his collect calls from jail. Mom usually accepted them, explaining she felt bad for him and that God was calling her to minister. The plastic waiting room chairs felt much bigger as a little girl. Her visits with Glen lasted awhile and it felt uncomfortable when the police officers stared at me from their check-in desk, as if I shouldn’t be there. After voicing these concerns to Mom, she had let me wait in the van. I didn’t mind going to the jail and even begged to join in efforts to stay near her. Even when she was inside, knowing her presence was close brought comfort.

Then, seeing my fake brown leather purse, there’s internal panic. Oh no! What if my purse smells like weed? Or what if I left weed in my purse and the officer finds it! My thoughts spiral and anxiety grows.

  “Ya, absolutely.”

Or maybe my suffocating anxiety made the room feel smaller. The officer sits across from me, and it appears Elizabeth has chosen her seat intentionally between me and the officer with a posture that claims protection.

And he would… lick me down there and have me give him head.” I have lost the words for oral sex and feel humiliated by how ridiculous this answer must sound.   “What?” My head is fuzzy, struggling to understand words.

For many years following I will wonder why victims are made to feel like the perpetrator during interrogations after disclosing abuse. I will also wonder how people develop a concept that the abused is responsible for destroying the perpetrators life when they go to prison, rather than understanding that Josiah made his own path the moment he chose to put his hands on me.

  I am not a little girl anymore.

  “Kelly,” his voice is soft. “Come here. Come down in the basement with me.”

  “Josiah, stop. I said I don’t want to.” My muscles are tight with irritation.
  “C’mon, Kelly, please. I promise it will be good.”

  The sun is starting to set by the time we leave the police station.
  “Well, that guy was not someone who should have been interviewing you for this. I don’t know what they were thinking putting you in a room to be questioned by him.” Elizabeth states the moment we are in the car.

  “Do you need to smoke?” Elizabeth asks, staying in tune to what I might need in the moment.

Shortly after sitting down, she takes a phone call from her husband. I listen, wondering what her family is like. She speaks of her kids often and I imagine her living the white picket fence life. Raising her children with love, structure, and routine while head over heels in love with her husband. A safe life where everyone's needs are met.

  “Oh, we can go if you need to get home. I can ask for a to-go container.”

  “Ya, I’m just not very hungry.”

  “I don’t know. I just really don’t want Josiah to get into trouble. And I don’t want my family to be mad at me.” I can’t come to terms with either of these concepts. Sending someone else to prison? I feel like the worst person in the world. Potentially losing my mom? More damage to that relationship feels unthinkable.

I have three boys and keep thinking that what if I had a daughter and one of my sons did this to her. Or what if one of my sons did this to another. My sons are my life and I love them more than anything, but if any of them did this to one of my other kids I would want justice and for them to have whatever consequences came. Even if that meant visiting them in prison or rehab or wherever else. There are consequences to the choices that we make.”
  “No, Elizabeth, you don’t understand. You think that right now, but if you were in this situation you would know that it’s not that easy. You can’t just choose between two of your kids.” These are my mom's words and I’m in complete denial of the fact that based on these responses, she has already chosen Josiah.
