Even if it were hard though, I know that part of loving my children is holding them accountable for their decisions, especially when they are this bad. If not, how would they ever learn? I would have a job to protect my other kids from farther harm even if it meant one of my kids having to learn the hard way. What he did to you was not okay. He hurt you. That is not your fault and he should not have done that. If one of my kids experienced this at the hands of one of my others, I would do whatever it took to protect that child and make sure the other got the help they needed, whatever that would look like.” Her eyes are honest and I want to believe her. She is desperate to reach me, working hard to help me see a different perspective. But she is just one person--who I barely know--standing against everyone else in my life speaking the opposite. She is challenging my entire world--a world in which the only righteous choice is to forgive and forget, even when men continually hurt people. A world in which parents feel bad for perpetrators because they must have had a hard life and female victims are told not to make a big deal of it. A world in which the truth will only set you free if you are talking about Christ’s salvation. But the truth of the sufferer should be concealed, hidden away, and allowed to fester as a wound forever.

Chapter 27

The Black Sheep


  A week later I arrive at the Children’s Advocacy Center for another interview. The building is a small, house-like structure located in a town near Stevensville. Shortly after I arrive alone, Mom shows up with Sarah, Daniel, and Avalon. While standing in the lobby waiting, I hug my siblings tightly, hoping that this simple gesture will communicate: I am so sorry for putting you in this position. At the time part of me held responsibility for the abuse, particularly the heavy weight of everything following my disclosing, a burden that would follow for many years. The idea of being the “black sheep” in the family proved more true than ever, validating feelings I had experienced my entire life.
  In the lobby, Mom approaches me and says something like: “You know Kelly, you don't have to talk. You don't have to say anything. Josiah could get into a lot of trouble if you do and we need to keep him out of jail.” Her comments send a flaming rage through my veins. Just like when I was a kid, my wellbeing means nothing in comparison to Josiah's. At this point, I am so desperate for the love of a parent, Mom could easily manipulate me by giving the slightest amount of love, empathy, support, or understanding. Yet I have received nothing but criticism, judgment, and demands to stay silent.
  I give mom an uncomfortable eye roll and walk to the other end of the room. Why isn't Mom being caring and supportive like Elizabeth is? I wonder. Why doesn’t she acknowledge that I have been hurt? Or that it was wrong? Why does she want me to keep this a secret? Especially considering her stance on lying being such a big sin? There are no answers for these questions. My family has put the weight of Josiah going to jail on my shoulders, yet everything feels so far out of my control, as it has my entire life.
  What I do know is that regardless of not wanting Josiah to go to jail, I won't survive keeping my secret any longer, for its heaviness would surely destroy me.
  Eventually, I am directed to a room down a hallway. This space is more welcoming than the police station reminding me of a church nursery with carpeted floors and soft colored wallpaper. I sit in a padded rocking chair beside a shelf of toys and across from me a woman rests in a rocking chair of her own. She introduces herself as some sort of social worker and for some reason her presence feels calming. On the wall beside her there’s a two sided mirror.
  “There are detectives on the other side of that mirror and this questioning will be recorded," she explains. Today, I remember nothing more about this interview, except that despite my mom waiting in the lobby nearby, I answered all of this woman’s questions truthfully.
  Shortly after leaving, my phone floods with texts from Mom. Apparently, the workers at the center have informed her that Daniel and Avalon denied any abuse. Sarah disclosed some, but not much. But I, Kelly, disclosed a lot of detailed sexual abuse that went on nearly daily for many years, including after the age of twelve years old. This was a very important detail because as we will soon learn, it adds an additional legal charge. Thinking back, I imagine the workers informed mom of these details in hopes it would give her additional perspective into the truth of the matter, but can’t help wondering if they might have acted differently had they known how mom would use this against me.
  After the interview at the Children’s Advocacy Center mom’s texts continue and even get worse than they have been. “I told you not to talk. What did you tell them? Josiah is going to jail and it’s all your fault. You’re a bitter person Kelly and you need to learn to forgive. You are destroying this family and need serious help.” The messages come in paragraphs, one after another, after another, after another. My head hurts not knowing how to calm Mom or help her understand. At seventeen years old I don’t have the coping tools to deal with this legal and emotional situation, much less help an adult to understand the gravity of the abuse. Rather than consoling me, Mom repeatedly accuses me of being angry and vengeful. This is confusing because I feel no anger--just fear, pain and confusion. So much confusion.
  Many years later, when Sarah, Avalon, and I reflect on these experiences, we all agree that at different points Mom told us to lie. Avalon states, "Mom told me to lie and I was terrified of her so I listened! I mean, Josiah never sexually abused me but I didn't tell them about the people who had!” Silence follows these conversations as we all internally reflect on the traumatizing experience of our mother telling us to lie about our abuse. There aren’t words to describe that heavy of a betrayal. “I will say though,” Avalon jumps back in “that the sexual abuse I was experiencing stopped after this. I think he got too scared after seeing what Josiah went through.” She would have been eleven years old at the time of my disclosing.
  Dad contacts me a few days after the Children’s Advocacy Center interview. For some reason, I feel a small sense of relief when he asks to meet and talk. As a child he favored me over Josiah and I imagine he will be angry that someone laid hands on his little girl.
  When I arrive at his home, he directs me toward the garage. It is a snowless December, cold and cloudy. During this conversation I observe that Sylvia stands a few feet behind dad, as if hiding in his shadows. “So Kelly, I need to know exactly what is going on here.” Dad demands.
  “Well, I went to talk to my teacher because I was looking for a counselor and when I told her that I was sexually abused by Josiah she needed to report it…” I finish speaking without a stutter. Having repeated the same story for what feels like one hundred times just this week, I could tell it in my sleep. But more importantly, I feel confident in Dad’s security.
  “When exactly did this happen?” His voice is stern.
  “I don’t remember when it started. It just always happened, ever since I could remember," I explain.
  “So you can’t tell me when exactly it happened?” His questions now feel accusing, as if to catch me in a lie.
  “I don’t know Dad. It happened regularly, all of the time.” I am desperate to convince him of my truth.
  “Are you sure you didn’t hallucinate it or make it up? You know, marijuana is a hallucinogen and you’ve been smoking that stuff. It can make you think all kinds of wonky stuff! You know your brother could get in all kinds of trouble for you just spewing words around like this, right?! That’s how serious this is!” His voice is rising and I feel shame as if he is speaking to me like a child. Is he joking? I am living this out, of course I know how serious it is.
  “No Dad, I d-d-d-idn’t. I d-d-didn’t… h-h-halluci-ci-cinate this. It actually happened.” My stutter is returning and I can’t make it stop. The accusations of me hallucinating feel ridiculous. How could someone think that I imagined such an experience?
  “Well, I also think that you have been doing cocaine, which can also make you want to lie and make stuff up, so I’m sure that could be the case too," he explains.
  “What?!” Now my voice is rising, defenses so high that the stutter disappears again. “Dad, I don’t do coke! I don’t even know what cocaine looks like aside from what I have seen in the movies! And marijuana doesn’t make someone hallucinate!” The marijuana one angers me, considering that I had once found Dad’s personal jar of it on a kitchen shelf. My eyes look to Sylvia, desperate for support. Instead, she refuses to make eye contact and remains silent.
  “Yes, you do, Kelly! I saw it in your eyes myself. That day you came to move out, your pupils were all dilated and you were acting all energetic, and I’ve seen that before. It was cocaine you were on. I’m sure of it!” He’s shouting now.
  “No Dad, it was Adderall that I took that day, which also doesn’t make someone hallucinate.” My face is on fire just having this conversation. Is he actually accusing me of making something like this up? Does he actually believe that my own memories from my entire childhood are inaccurate? I can’t make sense of this and won’t dare speak my true thoughts. I feel terrified and alone, as if the entire world is against me. The feeling reminds me of my nightmares about the end of the world from my childhood. Complete terror, lack of control, and aloneness. As if I’m evil and no matter my efforts to be good, corrupt is all I’ll ever be. Except there is no escaping or waking up from this real life nightmare.
  “Well, what about all of those times when you would bang on Josiah's door begging him to let you into his room back at the Concord house? Huh? You must have wanted it. If you didn’t want it to happen, you wouldn’t have hung around him. You wouldn’t have lived with him and Lesley a few years ago and you would have told your mom and I when you were a kid so that we could stop it. But you didn’t do that so you must have wanted it. You probably begged for it with the way you banged on Josiah's door.” I am helpless and small at the hands of his accusations, without the slightest clue how to defend myself. If he doesn’t believe my words, I don’t know what else to do.
  “Dad, I really don’t know what you’re talking about," I explain. “If I banged on Josiah's door it was probably because he was awful to me and took something of mine that I was trying to get back. Or maybe I did need him for something, but not this. I didn’t want this and I didn’t beg for it. I was embarrassed and didn’t want anyone to know. And I was scared of getting into trouble. I stayed with Josiah and Lesley because I wanted to stay in my school near friends and Josiah was in Iraq most of that time anyway.” My words are powerless and don’t give Dad the proof he is looking for. The conversation repeats a bit, then fizzles when neither of us back down.
  At the time I didn’t know that it’s impossible for people understand my experience if they aren’t willing to hear. Since my earliest memories the abuse was all I knew. I hadn’t even known that many kids don’t share the same experience of daily sexual abuse. Not only was it a normal part of my childhood, but I had feared that talking would get me into just as much trouble, if not more, than Josiah. After all, my biblical teachings and the adults in my life had drilled into me that sexual encounters outside of marriage were the worst type of sin. One that happened because a woman must be doing something to make the man lust after her. There also hadn’t been a safe person to tell even if I had mustered the courage to do so.
  A few days later, Dad asks to meet again. This time, I am smarter than to go alone and bring Jenn. When Dad answers the door, his eyes shift between us, confused. “This is Jenn. She’s my boss over at the restaurant. I hope you don’t mind that I brought her for support.” He sighs and I sense his discontent with me bringing an “outsider” for support.
  “Well, come on in, I guess," he says, stepping aside to create space. The house smells of the usual wood burning stove and cigarettes. We walk to the kitchen and stand awkwardly, no one knowing what to say.
  “Hi, I’m Jenn," Jenn begins introductions.
  Dad shifts on his feet, rubbing his hands through his hair as if in distress. Then, he speaks. “Can you, Jenn, wait in the other room? My wife and I need to talk to my daughter, alone.” It is a demand, not a question.
  Jenn is directed to a back bedroom to wait while dad, Sylvia, and I talk at the kitchen table.
  At first, Dad is silent, as if trying to collect himself. “So I’ve hired this lawyer,” he explains. “I scheduled a meeting and you will sit down and meet with him. He will tell you all of your rights and what to expect at the upcoming trial. These people, Kelly, these social workers and other attorneys that have been spewing all kinds of who-knows-what into your ears are going to make you feel like you have to talk. They are going to make you feel like you have to be angry and throw your brother in jail. But you don’t have to do that. Okay? You can go up on the stand and choose not to talk. That is your right. You can’t lie on the stand, but you can choose not to talk," he explains with his finger slamming against the table to make certain I understand the command.
  A trial has already been scheduled to take place within the next month and his demands feel like a threat, you will do this… or else… I don’t want to know what the or else part might be, but just want everything to end.
  Throughout this entire process, I was confused, insecure, terrified and shocked. There was so much pressure on me and expectations flying from all directions. Police and social workers drilled me for the most detailed descriptions of what had happened. Yet my family demanded I stay silent. I had stepped outside of the family norm and they expected me to get back in line, but I couldn’t. Getting back into line meant returning to the emptiness, fear and disgust I felt toward myself. Over the weeks individuals dismissing my experience made me want to talk even more. I had been shoved down so much that survival would come through an explosion of retaliation. The process may not have started from a place of anger or wanting revenge, but I felt forced into a corner and trapped. The only way out was to scream my truth.
  Immediately after leaving Dad’s, I face one glaring truth: my family isn’t safe. I am realizing that all along, the problem might not have been me, but them. When reflecting on my life, I realize that I have been shamed and silenced each time I have shared a truth or showed glimmers of my authentic self. I can't do it anymore because it’s destroying me. Suddenly, I know what I have to do.
  It is a particular feeling. A moment of clarity.
  The Holy Spirit, guardian angel, or maybe a personal intuition is speaking to me. A voice inside me that somehow knows the next right step. The one guiding me so well that I know everything will be okay if I just follow it. Something has to give and it won’t be me. Not this time. If saving myself means taking down the entire family, then that’s what I will do. I will do anything to shatter the horrific abuse dynamics and save myself, no matter the cost. Because sometimes, things need to be destroyed in order for something new, better, and more beautiful to grow.

~

  From what I know, Grandma Williams hasn’t learned of my disclosure yet and I don’t want to face her. Corbin moved out of Jenn’s earlier in the year and she has offered me their extra room.
  At my first availability, I move out of Grandma’s. She isn’t home when I pack, and I race the clock to avoid crossing paths. While gathering my belongings, I receive a text. This time, it is from Abe asking if he can come for a visit. Again, I hope, Yes, Abe! I am so relieved. He is the sibling who I am closest with who will truly get it. He will understand and hug me and tell me that everything is going to be okay. I breathe a huge sigh of relief.
  When he arrives, he does hug me, then asks what is going on. I again explain the series of events but this time feel confident sharing with someone in the family who will be on “my side”.
  He tries to empathize, then states, “You know Kelly, Josiah was sexually abused by Glen. He only did that because of what he went through.”
  “Yes, Abe, I know that. I feel bad about that. About all of this. I didn’t mean for any of it to happen. But, it happened, and I can’t lie when people ask me.” I sit on the edge of my bed at grandma’s, not knowing what else to say.
  “Kelly, you know you don’t have to talk. Josiah is facing serious charges and could go to jail for a long time. They are saying twenty-five to thirty years in prison, then life on parol and the sex offender list. His entire life will be over.”
  “And I’m sorry about that. But I don't know what else to do. This is out of my hands…”
  “You can do something. You can choose not to talk. I know many girls who have been sexually abused. There’s Laura, and Candice, and Melony, and so many more. They didn’t send their perpetrators to jail and they are all fine. You will be okay without sending him to jail too. I promise.” He is desperate for me to hear him, but I can’t agree.
  “Abe, that sounds horrible, and I seriously hope those girls are getting some sort of help.” Deep inside, my insecurity wonders how all of those girls he knows are fine while I am not. What does that mean about me? And what does it mean for this to be a normal thing for Abe and the surrounding culture?
  “Look, all I’m saying, is that you don’t have to send him to prison just because you’re pissed.”
  That is it. I can’t hear it again--the idea that I am a bad person trying to get revenge. People assume I am angry rather than acknowledging and validating my experience of pain. No one understands me.
  Now, I am short and to the point, implementing boundaries, a word that my new therapist has taught me. “I think that you should leave, Abe.”
  “Woah, I didn’t mean to make you mad.” Even if he doesn’t mean to make me mad, he is.
  “I know, but I am mad and you need to leave," I say again, feeling stone cold and numb.
  Shortly after he leaves, I receive a text from Josiah saying something like, “Hey Kelly, I am really sorry about everything that happened. Can we talk? They are talking about prison time and this is really serious.” I am on the living room floor now, frozen, with a dry mouth, trying to make sense of Josiah texting me.
  The police have given me clear instructions: “If Josiah reaches out to you, do not respond and do not have any contact with him.”
  A few minutes later, when I don’t respond, another text comes. “Kelly, I said I am sorry. Can we please talk? Please Kelly. I am really sorry. This is serious. Please Kelly.”
  I close his text and message Elizabeth; her responses always bring me comfort. When I tell her I haven’t replied, she writes, “Oh my gosh, you did the right thing. You are so brave and so strong. I am proud of you. You amaze me and are stronger than anyone I know. Even in the darkness, you shine bright. What are some thoughts and feelings you’re having?” She barely knows me, yet consistently loves me better than those who know me best, including my own mother. I cling tightly to her words as my new lifeline.
  At Jenn’s, I live with her daughters, Cassie and Sam, and their stepdad, Blake. The house is old and rundown, but still functioning and I sleep in a small room that houses the furnace. The home is poverty-stricken, but the people are safe. Jenn continues listening to me both at work and home. Cassie, my best friend, proves to be a big support. And Sam, who is in seventh grade, fills me in on all of her middle school drama. When the days end, Cassie sneaks to her boyfriends and Sam, fearing being upstairs alone, begs to sleep with me. She is a refreshing break from everything happening in my life. Most nights, we laugh hours into the night until our bellies hurt. Then we fall asleep talking about whether God or aliens are real. These relationships carry through the next years.
  One day, I receive a Facebook message from a cousin in Texas stating that she has heard about everything going on and wants to check in on me. She then explains that Grandma Williams told her about my disclosure.
  What? I wonder. That’s weird because Grandma hasn’t said a thing to me. I had thought that when Grandma learned the news, she would at least check in on me. Now I feel humiliated that she has spread the news all the way to Texas. Immediately, I text her to schedule a time for talking in person.
  Leading up to the meeting, I rehearse my lines, longing to continue being the brave, strong and bright shining girl that Elizabeth sees in me.
  Jenn needs me to drive Sam somewhere and due to time constraints, this requires that I bring her to my meeting at Grandma’s. I set her up in the living room with the television and meet Grandma in her sunflower themed kitchen. The sunflowers are everywhere--on the wallpaper, dish-ware, tablecloths, and wall decorations. I have always wondered the reason behind the sunflowers but never asked.
  For this conversation, I start. “Hey Grandma, so extended family has been reaching out to check if I am okay with everything going on. When I asked where they heard about it, it sounds like it has come from you. I appreciate that they are checking in but also do not want a lot of people to know about this. It is very personal to me and I am wondering if you could not tell anymore people until I can sort this out," I explain.
  The first words out of her mouth ingrain in my mind, never to be forgotten. “You can’t tell me what to do!"
  I try to express my feelings as non threatening as possible, yet fail. Her defenses are up.
  “Grandma, I’m not trying to tell you what to do. This is just really personal to me and I feel uncomfortable with so many people knowing what is happening," I explain further, but she cuts me off.
  “No, you can’t tell me what to do! I will talk to who I want, when I want, about what I want. And do you have any idea what you are putting your mom through? She called me crying, ready to take her own life over this! You should call her and apologize for putting her through this.” It is at this moment that I notice the pattern: Grandma sounds just like Mom.
  “Putting Mom through what? This isn’t about Mom. I am just trying to get help and if Mom is making this about her, then I don’t know how to stop that.” This is not in my script, but I also hadn’t anticipated such a response from Grandma. It feels like everyone expects me to care about their needs, but no one even acknowledges mine.
  “No, you are putting your mom through too much. If you are going to tear this family apart, then you can get out of my house right now!” She is shouting while walking toward the living room. She looks to Sam and continues. “And you too! Get out! Get out of my house. Kelly, you take your lowlife hoodlum friend and get out of my house! You are no longer welcome here!” Sam and I rush for our coats and boots while grandma follows us out the door shouting.
  Sam is only twelve years old, knowing little about my circumstances. I apologize profusely, even calling Jenn to explain the situation. “Had I known my grandma would react like that, I never would have brought Sam. I am so sorry Jenn.” Thankfully, she is understanding.
  When I return to my room at Jenn’s, I sit on the bed and call Elizabeth. It has been weeks and is the first time since disclosing that I cry. I cry so hard that I’m unable to catch my breath, hoping Elizabeth can take my pain away. I know she wishes the same, but she can’t. She can, however, continue speaking the same words of comfort, validating my feelings and reminding me I’m not alone.
  “I don’t have words Kelly. I am so sorry that you are going through this. It doesn’t make sense. You don’t deserve this. You deserve so much more. You are stronger than I ever could be and everyday I am amazed by you. You truly amaze me.” Thinking back, I don’t know that she will ever understand the comfort and strength she brought me during those moments.

~

  I’m not particularly close to Chase, but one night he ordered takeout from the restaurant while I worked. When picking the food up he said, “Kelly, why are you doing this?”
  I spit out some answers similar to what I had told the others then returned to work. His question was enough for me to know where he stood.
  As I cry to Elizabeth, it feels official. Aside from a few friends, disclosing my abuse has cost me every relationship. The damage is done and I am more aware of this than ever. There will be no turning back.
  Elizabeth's words bring safety, love, and peace, but they can’t take away this aching pain. Being vilified and detested by my entire family--my parents, my siblings, and even my grandmother--is too much to bear. I hangup from our conversation and head straight for the bathroom where I grab a new shaving razor and tear it apart. Once a single razor is free, I sit on the toilet and slit rows up my forearm. I don’t cut to kill myself. I don’t even cut because I want to hurt myself. Instead, the slices resemble the wounds I have experienced at the hands of others. Feeling them in my heart is one thing, but I need to see them. “This cut is from Abe. This one is from Mom. This one is Grandma… and Shannon… and Dad…and Josiah...” Continuing down the list, I wonder if they even care about the pain they have caused. Surprisingly, I find the experience grounding. Over the next months and years each time I feel the tight slits under my shirt, whether I be in bed, at school, or work, It’s my reminder not to trust. Yes, I feel you. You are hurting. They caused you this pain. I remember and I won’t forget.
  When I finish, I head to bed and pray my new nightly prayer. “Lord, I don’t have anything left in me. If I wake tomorrow, it has to be you getting me out of bed, because I can’t do it. There is nothing left of me. It all just hurts so much. Please Lord, let me die. Whether it be a car accident or sickness or some other wound, please, if you’re real, just let me die. Have mercy on me by letting death save me from this pain. It’s too much. Please Lord, if you could do me this one favor and just take me away from this world…” I beg harder than I have ever begged for anything, as I lack strength to take my own life. This prayer continues until sleep overtakes me. For years to follow, it remains my mantra.

Chapter 28

Family Matters


  Christmas comes with more pain. My parents quickly pay Josiah's bail for his first arrest, then Mom directly informs me that I will not be welcome to Grandma Williams’ for Christmas dinner.
  “This might be our last Christmas with Josiah for a long time and we want to spend it together," she explains, attempting to justify her actions for choosing him over me. When Christmas is finally important to mom, I’m not welcome. What I didn’t know at the time is that this would be the first of many holidays I would be uninvited to, until one day the invitations would stop coming all together.
  The entire family, even extended family, which I would later learn, always had excuses for him. Whether it be past trauma, his current circumstances of facing jail time, or his “good character” for serving in Iraq, there was always something. I was the worldly child who smoked marijuana, had too many emotions, didn’t respect my elders, and chose to expose family secrets. All of this meant I had no place in the family. Bending to their wishes would mean abandoning myself. The cost was too high.
  When Elizabeth learns that I’ve been uninvited for family Christmas, she invites me to her own parents’ home for dinner and a gathering. She has known me four months as a student, but only one on a more personal level. Within that short time, it seems she has seen enough to know the extent of my family’s dysfunction. Thinking back it would have been easy for her to walk away, wishing me the best. I imagine that most people would have feared what could come with my family’s unpredictable tendencies and done just that. Instead, she invited me deeper into her life, choosing to join my journey into the darkness. There are still no words for the amount of gratitude I have from these acts of love and acceptance.
  Elizabeth’s words of support and encouragement continue, now including stuff like, “You continue to amaze me. You have such good perspectives and are wise beyond your years. You will go far in this world. I know it doesn’t feel like it right now, but one day you will have full control over your life and you will live nothing less than the life that you want. You will find what you’re looking for.” She is speaking words of hope, reminding me that the situation will pass. My life can one day be whatever I want it to be.
  I feel shame and embarrassment for accepting an invitation to something as intimate as family Christmas, but am more scared of being alone--the thoughts are too dark there.
  Her parents' home is older, yet well-kept, reminding me of Grandma Reed’s house. The inside air is warm, cozy, and smells of a freshly cooked meal. She introduces me to her husband, Jake. Similar to her, he is tall, with blonde hair and blue eyes. Their kids are just as she described them. Logan, age eight, with wavy blond hair. Nathan, age five, with curly red hair. Lastly, Ezra, eleven months old, completely bald. The home is also filled with aunts, uncles, cousins, and grandparents. Everyone welcomes me with smiles and open arms.
  As thankful as I am for a place to spend the holiday, it isn’t home. At points throughout the night I feel panic followed by an instinct to run away. Then, the thoughts, I will just go home. I will apologize to my family for everything and tell them that they are right, I am a selfish bitter person who made all of this up for attention. I will tell them I am so sorry and won’t do it again. Things will go back to normal and it will be like none of this ever happened. Then I remember, there is no “undoing” my disclosure to law enforcement.
  Elizabeth’s gift to me is a pink puffer vest and a small cross with the quote “You are so loved” written on the back. With it there is a sweet note, inspiring courage and hope. For the first time in almost eighteen years, someone sees me. It feels as if Elizabeth has reached the little girl inside of me and is saying, I see all of these broken pieces that people keep smashing down. They are heavy. Here, let me pick them up and hold them with you. Because they are you and they are beautiful. Her unwavering love gives me strength to continue taking each next step forward.
  The idea that just when things can’t get any worse, they do feels more true than ever. Just days after Christmas my friend, Chrystal, gets pulled over by the police for having a license plate light out while driving to a New Year’s Eve party. Even the reason she has gotten pulled over feels ridiculous. A license plate light? Really? I hadn’t even know those were a thing until this night. After letting her off with a warning, the police officer looks to me and asks “Miss, how old are you?”
  “I’m seventeen,” I reply, not paying much attention.
  “Then why do I see a pack of cigarettes in your purse?”
  When he asks for my drivers license, I provide it without speaking a word, unable to even look at him. Although I will be of legal age in less than two months, he writes me a ticket and I am scheduled for a court date. I want to scream at him, “Do you have any idea what I am going through?! Do you?! Because if you did you would know that the least thing I deserve is a fucking cigarette.” But the words stick in my throat. I don’t want another court date but getting off out of pity feels worse.
  Chrystal speaks after putting the car in drive, but I can’t hear the words. Instead, the panic comes full force. “Help! I need out of this car. I need out. Right now! I need out! I need out! Get me out!” My hands are hitting the door next to me, as if this will get me out of the car. Then, I find the door handle and pull repeatedly, floundering for the lock and failing to find it.
  We are going nearly fifty-five miles per hour down a back country road, but my brain can’t process being in a moving car. It just needs space from the world caving in on me.
  “Woah, Kelly! What is happening?! What are you doing? Don’t open the door! The car is moving!” Chrystal screeches.
  Something about the panic in her voice brings sense back into me. “I just need air! Please, pull over! I need air! I just need air!” I’m dizzy and drop my head between my legs, hoping this will help my breathing. I’m not sure why I do this, other than that my mom used to do it to stop from passing out on occasion.
  When she finally parks in a lot, I scramble from the car and fall to the ground crying. It is heavy and uncontrollable, then silent choking cries once I can know longer breathe.
  Eventually, after I don’t know how long, the winter wind thrashes at my face, pulling me back into the moment. The more I focus on the freezing of my tears, the easier my breaths become until finally, things slow. I lean against the car, exhausted, not wanting to leave the cool air.
  Eventually, Chrystal approaches me, asking about next steps. She is a coworker and classmate but knows little of my family circumstances. I am desperate to not ruin her night because of my episode. After pulling my weak body to its feet, I wipe the tears and we finish our drive to the New Year’s Eve party. The party is noisy, dark, and overcrowded. As the designated driver, I refuse to drink, but do get high, hoping to relax enough for my company to be more enjoyable for others. On this night, it doesn’t work. I feel tense and my laughs are fake. People speak to me but I struggle to process their words, anxious to return home to Jenn’s.

~

  That next week I meet with Dad’s attorney. Prior to the meeting, I receive a call from my older brother, Jacob. “Hey, I just want to check in and see how you are doing.” Being eleven years older than me, he moved out when I was only seven years old, so I don’t know him well, aside from the rare occasions Mom took us to visit him in Grand Rapids where he lived. I dread another family interaction, but am pleasantly surprised when--after I explain the situation with Josiah, the attorney and everything else--he responds with, “I am trying to think of ways I can be there for you. How can I support you?”
  This is unlike any interaction I have had with family members. How can he support me? I haven’t had to think about that and don’t even know how to answer this question, I think to myself. My eldest brother, James had also reached out to me. Although James offered support, my guard remained up. He had taken the route of sharing some of his own childhood struggles and I didn’t know how to connect in that way. Not with a man.
  Jacob’s response doesn’t include any of that. He doesn’t offer advice or shared experience. Similar to Elizabeth, he meets me in my pain and trusts me to know what I need. By the time we finish talking, he has agreed to come with me to speak with the lawyer.
  Per Dad's instructions, we all meet at his house, then ride together. Upon arrival the attorney explains “I need to speak with her alone.” Dad disagrees, but the lawyer won’t have it. Finally, with a tight jaw, he cooperates.
  Now, I am alone, sitting across the desk from a man with a fancy suit. “Okay, so I want to hear from you what has been going on?” he asks.
  When I finish explaining the situation he takes a deep breath and leans back in his leather swivel seat. “You know, your father is right. By law, you are not required to talk if you don’t want to. But sitting here, listening to you, I believe, with one hundred percent certainty, that you should go up on that stand and speak your truth. Don’t hold back. There’s no reason to be silent for a situation like this. That is my professional opinion.” For the first time since being in his office, I glance up, needing to make sure he is genuine.
  Back in the car, Dad is desperate to hear how the meeting went. He wiggles in his seat repeating, “Okay Kelly, so what did he say? He told you your rights, correct? That you don’t have to talk. Right? What did he say?” It is when he turns to face me that I notice the new drawn lines on his face. It is as if he has aged ten years overnight and dark eye circles hang heavy. He no longer appears scary, just drained, empty, worn down and weary. But in that, I see hope peaking through, as if this meeting with the attorney will somehow change Josiah's fate.
  My voice is barely above a whisper, “Well, Dad, he told me that I should talk. He said that I have a right to choose not to speak but that he is confident I should go on the stand and talk about my abuse.”
  Unable to look at me, his eyes now seem distant as he stares out the front window. After a deafening silence, he asks, “So what are you going to do?”
  “I’m going to talk,” is all I say.
  It feels as if my family has silenced me my entire life. “Kelly, you’re being too loud. Shut up.” Or “Gosh Kelly, you’re so annoying. Just stop talking.” Or “You are a liar and do everything for attention. It’s so annoying.” Or “Your voice is hurting my ears.” Finally, I have people who are not only willing to listen, but are strongly encouraging me to speak. It is empowering and validates complex emotions that I have felt for many years.
  It is a silent drive back to Dad’s. When we arrive, his head falls to the steering wheel as he sobs. His tears are a desperate plea and through them he repeats out loud “What about grace? What about mercy? Where is that in all of this? Huh? Mercy? Where is mercy for my son?” I don’t know if the pleas are for me or God, but the emotions are suffocating.
  The only time I have ever seen Dad cry prior to this was three years before when Mom and George went on their honeymoon. I stayed with him for three weeks and nearly every night, he wept at his computer desk, grieving the loss of his marriage. I sat near him, wanting to provide comfort but not knowing how. The tears he has today are worse.
  I step out of the car, feeling the sole responsibility for my family’s pain. Jacob meets me outside the car and wraps his arms around me for a tight hug. “What do you need? Should I come with you?” he asks.
  “No, I will be fine. It looks like he needs you more than I do right now. I just want to be alone," I explain, truthfully. People, no matter who they are, feel exhausting.
  By this point, I’m second-guessing everything. Even having a basic conversation triggers anxiety. Did I say something that could be used against me? Do they think I’m lying? Am I showing too much emotion? Not enough emotion? My brain is at war. I just want to smoke my cigarettes and marijuana alone while re-reading all of Elizabeth’s texts until they feel true. That feels safe.
  The following Monday night, Elizabeth meets me at the courthouse. We are to talk with the prosecutor and discuss what to expect in the court hearing, which is scheduled for the following morning. We are both tense, neither of us knowing what to expect.
  The prosecutor is a woman wearing business clothes with a tight bun in her hair. She starts by stating “So, good news! I just got off the phone with Josiah and he has agreed to accept the plea bargain.”
  Elizabeth and I look at each other, then she asks, “Okay, so what does this mean?”
  “Well, I am not sure what changed his mind, especially this last minute. Earlier today he was set on going to trial, but this means you no longer need to testify tomorrow! The two charges for you and your sister under the age of twelve will be dropped and only the one when you were thirteen and older will remain. He will be sentenced four months in jail, six years of probation, and will be on the sex offender list for life,” she explains.
  Everyone in the room breathes sighs of relief. “That’s good, right Kelly? This is what you wanted?” Elizabeth looks at me, knowing my biggest fear has been Josiah getting a serious charge and my family hating me for it.
  “Ya, this is good. It is!” I attempt to convince myself.
  I don’t mean it. I had hoped that a smaller sentence would enable my family to love and accept me again but at this point, know that is unlikely. Deeper inside, I’m having unspeakable thoughts, How did his sentence just go from twenty-five years in prison to four months in jail? Is fifteen years of sexual abuse only worth that? How did his charges for abusing me and Sarah under the age of twelve, the majority of the abuse, get dropped? Does the court think I am overreacting and that this wasn’t a big deal too? How can they just drop two charges? It doesn’t make sense and over the following months, I will learn that Elizabeth feels just as confused. Every so often, a comment, such as, “I still can’t believe he got such a ridiculous plea bargain. It doesn’t make sense," slips from her. When this happens, she changes the topic quickly, as if not wanting to put her own emotion from the experience onto me.
  “You can still come to court tomorrow, but you don’t have to.” The prosecutor continues.
  “Oh, I guess I am… I still want to go. I want to be there," I state.
  “Are you sure?” They both look surprised.
  “Ya, I’m sure. I want to go just in case," I explain.
  Nobody asks for clarification about what “just in case” means. I hadn’t entered into this situation angry, but my family’s responses have left a boiling rage in me. One that I now direct toward Josiah. He knows this is true! And still, he’s going to stand by while the family treats me like this. He’s going to watch me suffer rather than tell the family to back down. For some reason, I assumed he had more humanity in him than that. It seems that he has accepted the plea bargain for fear of the trial not leaning in his favor rather than because he takes genuine accountability for his actions. I hide these thoughts and feelings though, refusing to be what my family thinks of me.
  Today, many years later, I confess. What it meant was “just in case I still get the chance to testify”. As terrified as I was, some part of me wanted to speak in court. He could lie to everyone, but I imagined staring him straight in the eyes during my testimony, showing him my power. He would look at me and we would both know the truth of it all. I would be taking back full control of my body and life.
  This plea bargain, similar to my family’s responses, was a kick in the fucking gut.
  During this time, Elizabeth introduces me to another “support”, Angela. Elizabeth is unable to make the court date, so Angela comes in her place. She is a middle-aged mom, working toward her master’s degree in social work. Similar to Elizabeth, she has a gentle and compassionate spirit. One day, I remember her saying, “You are so resilient. I am amazed by it.” She then had to explain what the word “resilient” meant, as I had never heard it before. After she did, she held her hand in a fist. “You see this?” She said. “There is so much darkness right now. And you are in the midst of a long journey. But this hand, right here, that is going to resemble a light at the end of this dark tunnel. Because light is coming. It always does. You are not alone and I am going to be right here, helping you search for this light," she explained. It was as if she were becoming another person in my corner.
  Even more amazing, was the reality that Elizabeth, Angela and Cynthia--who I would later meet--brought the same feeling of comfort and safety that I had when praying as a little girl. A feeling that everything was good and nothing evil could touch any of us. We were safe, protected, and strong. It was as if something spiritual were pulling us together for a greater purpose.
  Upon arriving to court, Angela sees Uncle Chad, who my dad brought for support. She turns to me and says, “Oh my gosh Kelly, Chad Cole. You are related to Chad Cole? I knew that I recognized the name Cole!”
  It turns out, she knows him from church and other local community circles. My dad’s brother, Uncle Chad and his wife, Aunt Lydia--the ones we spend Thanksgiving with--have always been the most “normal” people in our entire family. Chad is Dad’s brother and the spitting image of him in physique, except Uncle Chad never gives off unkind vibes and Aunt Lydia is sweet as they come. Even their adult children are successful. I make a joke in reply, “Ya, that’s my uncle. I was so close to having a normal childhood, right?” Thinking back, I wonder why they chose to stand by dad’s side rather than than checking in with me.
  Later, I will learn that Angela lived one street over from Grandma Reed. You can nearly see Grandma’s house from her backyard. Between Angela running in the same circles as my family and Elizabeth attending the Christian School with my brothers, all of us coming together at this moment felt like divine intervention. It was as if God’s angels, Elizabeth and Angela were close to me, even as a little girl, just waiting for this awestruck moment. Many years later, I would even learn that Angela had met Iva Gilligan, who ran that odd bible quizzing event I attended as a child. All along, my safe people were so close, yet somehow so far away. Today, I take comfort in the safety of their closeness, even as a little girl.
  When entering the courtroom, I see my parents and older brothers on Josiah's side, but Jacob stands in the back of the room as a neutral observer.
  They don’t need me to testify, but the attorneys circle, having a whispered argument and then gesture for me to come forward. My head feels light and my mouth goes dry as I approach. “We need to know the exact date of the last time he tried to touch you," they explain.
  This debate over dates of sexual abuse incidences has been happening between lawyers, my family, and me for weeks. My parents and Abe don’t believe my answer, no matter how hard I try to prove my honesty. The one date people fixate on, is when Josiah tried touching me in Grand Rapids at James and Monica’s house following his twenty-first birthday celebration. Lawyers, parents, and siblings have decided that if they are going to find flaws in my disclosure--if they are going to prove Josiah's innocence--it will be because I could have gotten this specific date wrong. If they can prove that, then I could be dismissed as a liar, making this up just to get someone in trouble. My family and their lawyers don’t care about my wellbeing. It is a strategy to discredit me and make Josiah into a hero who survived his own abuse.
  I had thought Mom’s mind fucks were bad, but this court experience is horrific. I had feared that people would be disgusted with my disclosure, as even I was repulsed with myself about the abuse. But I never imagined people wouldn’t believe me. Or that people were capable of going through such lengths to bury my memories. It is no wonder women refuse to come forward about their abuse. Because this court experience created more mental trauma for me than any of the others combined. I didn’t matter. My story didn’t matter. My wellbeing didn’t matter. My memories and experiences didn’t matter. The system's goal, just like my family, was to prove me “crazy” to protect another white male. I went from being trapped in the family to being trapped by the system. They asked for the most explicit details then turned on me, ripping me open just to bury me. The ultimate mind fuck.
  “Josiah did not celebrate his twenty-first birthday in Grand Rapids," they declare. I attempt to explain that although it might not have been a twenty-first birthday exactly, he did go to the bars with them so he was twenty-one. And the movie The Hangover played that night, which I fact checked and had been released that same year. These are details I give to prove myself, but I shouldn’t have needed to.
  “Uh, I already told you. It was around his twenty-first birthday," I explain.
  “Okay, but when exactly? We need a date. An exact date," the lawyers state.
  “I don’t know. I don’t have an exact date. I just know that it was his twenty-first birthday and he came back drunk and watched ‘The Hangover’ and tried later that night.” Eventually, they accept my answer and direct me toward my seat. The entire process has been a nightmare and I again feel vilified by the court system. Just like with my family, I wonder Why don’t they just believe me?
  Josiah accepts the plea bargain and will begin his sentence in February, the following month.
  They all got what they want and the worst is over. Now we can move on, I hope. Instead, what follows will be many years of continued emotional abuse based on this event. My family swarms to protect Josiah and accuses me of “destroying” his life by “putting him on the sex offender list”. My family still shames me for getting “revenge” yet has taken it upon themselves to punish me forever. Life is forever changed. No turning back.

PART 3

Ascension
